  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_44eb65f4-65fd-49ea-b5e0-1779a5525c6a'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer9596905165179141901">Otuz iki yıllık bir zaman diliminde yazılan İnce Memed dörtlüsü düzene başkaldıran Memed'in ve insan ilişkileri, doğası ve renkleriyle Çukurova'nın öyküsüdür. Yaşar Kemal'in söyleyişiyle "içinde başkaldırma kurduyla doğmuş" bir insanın, "mecbur adam"ın romanı.<br/><br/>Abdi Ağa'nın zulmüyle köyünü terk etmek zorunda kalan Memed, Ağa'nın yeğeniyle evlendirilmek üzere olan Hatçe'yi ka</span>
  <span id="freeText9596905165179141901" style="display:none">Otuz iki yıllık bir zaman diliminde yazılan İnce Memed dörtlüsü düzene başkaldıran Memed'in ve insan ilişkileri, doğası ve renkleriyle Çukurova'nın öyküsüdür. Yaşar Kemal'in söyleyişiyle "içinde başkaldırma kurduyla doğmuş" bir insanın, "mecbur adam"ın romanı.<br /><br />Abdi Ağa'nın zulmüyle köyünü terk etmek zorunda kalan Memed, Ağa'nın yeğeniyle evlendirilmek üzere olan Hatçe'yi kaçırır. Abdi Ağa'yı yaralayan, yeğenini de öldüren Memed eşkıya Deli Durdu'ya katılır, ancak kıyıcılığına katlanamadığı Deli Durdu'dan iki arkadaşıyla birlikte ayrılır. Memed, sıradan bir köy çocuğuyken, zulmedenler için eşkıyaya, köylüler içinse bir kurtarıcıya dönüşür. <br /><br />"Bir yaşam biçimini bir halkın portresi olarak böylesine veren bu romandan daha iyisi yazılamazdı." <br />- The New York Times Book Review, (A.B.D.) <br /><br />"Şaşırtıcı, orijinal bir kitap." <br />- Sunday Times, (İngiltere) <br /><br />"Epik boyutlara ulaşan ve muhteşem bir sona ulaşmak için hız kazanan öyküye kendinizi kaptırıyorsunuz." <br />- Sunday Times, (İngiltere) <br /><br />"Yaşar Kemal, şaşılacak ölçüde yaratıcı."<br />- The Bookseller, (İngiltere)<br /><br />"Yaşar Kemal, karakterlerini unutulmaz, seçkin ve gerçek hayattan daha da gerçekçi kılan detay zenginliği ile Rus edebiyatının kalitesine ulaşıyor."<br />- Sunday Telegraph, (İngiltere)</span>
  <a data-text-id="9596905165179141901" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<div id="review_21167266" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21167266" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21167266?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590171394" href="/book/show/175545.Memed_My_Hawk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=turkish">turkish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21167266" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13913308226880680248">Memed, My Hawk (İnce Memed, #1), Yaşar Kemal <br/><br/>Memed, My Hawk is a 1955 novel by Yaşar Kemal. It was Kemal's debut novel and is the first novel in his İnce Memed tetralogy. <br/><br/>The novel won the Varlik prize for that year (Turkey's highest literary prize) and earned Kemal a national reputation. In 1961, the book was translated into English by Edouard Roditi, thus gaining Kemal his first exposure to English-speaking readers. <br/><br/>Memed, a young boy from a village in Anatolia, is abused and beaten by the vil</span>
  <span id="freeText13913308226880680248" style="display:none">Memed, My Hawk (İnce Memed, #1), Yaşar Kemal <br /><br />Memed, My Hawk is a 1955 novel by Yaşar Kemal. It was Kemal's debut novel and is the first novel in his İnce Memed tetralogy. <br /><br />The novel won the Varlik prize for that year (Turkey's highest literary prize) and earned Kemal a national reputation. In 1961, the book was translated into English by Edouard Roditi, thus gaining Kemal his first exposure to English-speaking readers. <br /><br />Memed, a young boy from a village in Anatolia, is abused and beaten by the villainous local landowner, Abdi Agha. Having endured great cruelty towards himself and his mother, Memed finally escapes with his beloved, a girl named Hatche. <br /><br />Abdi Agha catches up with the young couple, but only manages to capture Hatche, while Memed is able to avoid his pursuers and runs into the mountains. There he joins a band of brigands and exacts revenge against his old adversary. Hatche was then imprisoned and later dies. <br /><br />When Memed returns to the town, Hatche's mother tells him he has a "women's heart" if he surrenders himself. He instead rides into town to find his enemy, on a horse given to him by the townspeople. He finds Agha in the south-east corner of his house and shoots him in the breast. The local authorities hear the gunshots, but Memed gets away. Before Hatche dies she gives birth to Memed's son, who is also named Memed. The protagonist then must take care of his son.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: سال 1975 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: اینجه ممد؛ نویسنده: یاشار کمال؛ مترجم: ثمین باغچه بان؛ تهران، کتاب زمان، 1353؛ در 515 ص؛ چاپ سوم: امیرکبیر، زمان، 1357؛ چاپ دیگر: تهران، ابتکار، 1362؛ در 515 ص؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان ترکیه - سده 20 م<br /><br />مترجم: امیر اسماعیلی؛ تهران، نشر اروند، 1362؛ در 216 ص؛ <br /><br />مترجم: خسرو مهرزاد؛ تهران، سمیر، 1382؛ در 755 ص؛ <br /><br />اینجه ممد، نخستین جلد، از دوره ی رمان چهار جلدی، اثر: یاشار کمال، نویسنده ی ترک است، که نخستین بار در سال 1955 میلادی، به صورت پاورقی در روزنامه ی جمهوریت، چاپ استانبول، منتشر شد. اینجه یعنی باریک، نرم، لاغر، ترکه باریک، نازک، ظریف و ...؛ کتاب در ترکیه دوازده بار تا سال 1977 میلادی، چاپ، و در سایر کشورها: ایران سالهای 1964؛ و ...؛ فرانسه سال 1961 میلادی، بریتانیا: سال 1961، بلغارستان در سال 1957؛ میلادی، شوروی: سال 1959 میلادی، چکسلواکی و یوگسلاوی: سال 1966 میلادی؛ رومانی سالهای 1964 و 1967 میلادی، مجارستان سال 1963؛ میلادی، ایتالیا: سال 1961 میلادی، اسپانیا: 1962 میلادی، پرتغال: سال 1964 میلادی، هلند: سال 1964 میلادی، آلمان غربی: از سال 1962 تا 1965 میلادی سه بار، آلمان شرقی: سال 1962 میلادی، اتریش: سال 1963 میلادی، آذربایجان: سال 1967 میلادی، ازبکستان: سال 1968 میلادی، هندوستان: سال 1962 میلادی، مولداویا: سال 1967 میلادی، سوئد: سال 1970 میلادی، و نروژ: در سال 1971 میلادی بارها چاپ شده است <br /><br />در جلگه ­ای در دامنه­ های کوه توروس، در دهکده ­ای که همه زمینهای آن متعلق به آقا (ارباب) محلی است، نوجوانی ممد نام زندگی می­کند، که او را اینجه (قلمی) می­نامند. ارباب هرسال اجاره ی سنگینی از زمینها میگیرد، و روستاییان را به حال خود رها می­کند، تا در فقر زندگی کنند. ممد چند بار فرار میکند، و خدیجه را هم که عاشقش است، همراه خود می­برد و ... اما در باره مترجم: ترجمه ی بسیار خوبی است از ثمین باغچه بان، به یاد پدرش «جبار باغچه بان»، بابا برفی، که تا روزی که تن برفیش در برابر آتش تنور، بدل به نقطه ای شود، پاروی هزار منی شاطریش را از دست ننهاد. ثمین باغچه بان. ا. شربیانی</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6604701274478127737">One of my criteria for rating a piece of fiction is this: Would I, or have I read it more than once. No matter how good, I almost never watch a movie more than once. But some books, I come back to over and over. This is such a book. I have read it several times. For me it is always entertaining and moving.   <span id="freeText6604701274478127737" style="display:none">One of my criteria for rating a piece of fiction is this: Would I, or have I read it more than once. No matter how good, I almost never watch a movie more than once. But some books, I come back to over and over. This is such a book. I have read it several times. For me it is always entertaining and moving. Set in Turkey, and written by a Noble Prize nominee, it is a kind of Robin Hood saga. <br />I tell all my students that if they wish to understand the prophetic passion for social justice that is to be found in the Old Testament, read Memed, My Halk. One is constantly outraged by the too real injustice suffered by Memed and the uncommon heroism of Memed as he fights and rises above it. <br />The translation from Turkish is excellent. This little known book deserves to be better known. Its author deserves to be more widely read.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2194506376" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2194506376" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4086118-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567611029p2/4086118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2194506376?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4086118-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590171394" href="/book/show/175545.Memed_My_Hawk">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Michael by: </span>
      Baran باران 
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=rural-life">rural-life</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=turkey">turkey</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=farming">farming</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2194506376" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10616207406924572987">This modern classic was first published in 1955. The tale is of a boy Ince Memed growing up in a rural village on central Anatolia who escapes the tyranny of a brutal local landlord by becoming a bandit hiding out in the rough country of the Taurus mountains. We experience him developing into a legend with overtones of Robin Hood and facing moral challenges to the good heart we can’t help rooting for, all the while fearing an end in a shootout like Bonnie and Clyde. The story is told with a subl</span>
  <span id="freeText10616207406924572987" style="display:none">This modern classic was first published in 1955. The tale is of a boy Ince Memed growing up in a rural village on central Anatolia who escapes the tyranny of a brutal local landlord by becoming a bandit hiding out in the rough country of the Taurus mountains. We experience him developing into a legend with overtones of Robin Hood and facing moral challenges to the good heart we can’t help rooting for, all the while fearing an end in a shootout like Bonnie and Clyde. The story is told with a sublime lyrical rendering of this place and people where Kemal grew up in a family of Kurdish immigrants. He captures the beauty so well of this arid high plains in the mountain foothills. The rhythms of village life among the subsistence farmers and herders of the region are portrayed so vividly with all the senses--sight, sound, touch, and smell. Every action feels larger than life, reaching for a new mythology to give meaning to the human struggle. <br /><br />We first encounter Memed at about 12 running away over a ridgeline to seek refuge with a farmer at a neighboring village. He tells of his life as a virtual slave to Abdi Agha, who owns all the land of five villages and cruelly lords it over its residents:<br /><i>For two years I’ve plowed his fields. The thistles devour me. They bite me. Those thistles tear at your legs like a mad dog. That’s the sort of field I plowed. Every day Abdi Agha beat me, beat me to death. … My father was dead and Abdi Agha took what little we had away from us. If my mother complained, he beat her cruelly and would beat me too. Once he tied me to a tree and left me there in the middle of the plain, far from the village. I stayed tied to the tree for two days, till Mother came and freed me. But for her the wolves would have torn me to pieces.</i><br /><br />He is captured and returned to work and punishment, but the dream of escape is awakened, and he bides his time. But Agha takes the majority of their grain crop for his own, and it is only though the charity of an old bandit living anonymously in their region that they avoid starvation. At 16, he falls in love with a sweet, industrious village girl, Hatche. Their love becomes part of legend that will develop about Memed after he becomes a bandit:<br /><i>Every night, whatever happened, they would meet. If not, neither of them could sleep at all. Hatche’s mother once caught them and punished her daughter. It was no use. …She put lock after lock on her door; Hatche found a way around every obstacle. She knitted stockings and kerchiefs for Memed and invented songs over them, expressing love, desire, and jealousy in the colors of her embroidery and in the notes of her songs that are still sung throughout the Taurus. People who saw her stockings were thrilled, and those who hear or sing her songs still feel a thrill like the freshness of spring when everything is green.</i><br /><br />People who see Memed in this period sometimes become aware of the rebellion lurking in his spirit:<br /><i>All his life and energy, his hate, love, courage, and anxiety were concentrated in his big eyes. Every now and then a tiny spark would light them up and then die, a sharp, piercing spark, to be feared like the spark that flickers briefly in the eye of a tiger ready to pounce and tear its prey. Where does this spark come from? Perhaps one is born with it. More likely it is born of torment, pain, anxiety. It had come to stay in Memed’s eyes in the past year, though the light of wonder and pleasure had always glistened in his childish gaze before.</i><br /><br />Once Memed makes his escape, he comes to be part of a new tribe of sorts and has to negotiate a tough path among many bloodthirsty and lawless men. While some of their leaders are cruel and murderous, he eventually inspires others toward a less brutal ethos and hope of liberating his home region from Agha’s oppression. His girlfriend is imprisoned for helping him escape, and the dream of freeing her and living a normal life dwells deep in his heart. As larger and larger police contingents pursue him in his mountain hideout, his plans must become even bolder to achieve his aims. Good deeds rendered to others along his life journey are paid back in propitious ways.<br />I love how it slowly dawns on Memed that life is better elsewhere in the larger world. Not only might there be places free from tyrant landlords and with easier farming of loamy, well-watered fields free of thistles, there are cities beyond his imagination. A man he encounters puts this vision in his mind:<br /><br /><i>There’s a city there, Adana, all of clear glass. It sparkles day and night, just like the sun. You walk in the alleys between the houses, they call them streets, and it’s all glass. It’s as clean as can be. Trains come and go. On the sea, ships as big as villages go to the other end of the world. Everything shimes like the sun, bathed in light. If you look at it just once, you can’t take your eyes away. If it’s money you want, it pours like a flood in the Chukurova. All you’ve got to do is work.</i><br /><br />In Kemal’s introduction, he explains his fascination with people who are destined to become rebels committed to a cause:<br /><i>I have never believed in heroes. Even in those novels in which I focus on revolt I have tried to highlight the fact that those we call heroes are in effect instruments wielded by the people. The people create and protect those instruments and stand or fall together with them. …That is because there is a germ of revolt within every human being, just as there is a creative power, and it is this feeling of revolt that produces the “committed person.”<br /><br />What I understood was that when people find themselves cornered, when they feel the pain of death in their heart, they tend to create a world of myth in which they try to take refuge. By creating myths, by conjuring up worlds of dreams, one can withstand the great suffering of the world and attain love, friendship, beauty, and, even perhaps, immortality.</i><br /><br />I was greatly moved by this mini-epic with Homeric ovetones. I thank Goodreads friend Baran for recommending this book and look forward to continued exploration of readings in Turkish literature and history.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10616207406924572987" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1508930587" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1508930587" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1895570-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484874117p2/1895570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1508930587?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/1895570-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590171394" href="/book/show/175545.Memed_My_Hawk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=middle-east">middle-east</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1508930587" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12387656727720561219"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14556509.Yashar_Kemal' title='Yashar Kemal' rel='nofollow noopener'>Yashar Kemal</a> is probably the best known author from that most admirable of Middle-Eastern peoples: The Kurds. His <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/175545.Memed__My_Hawk___nce_Memed___1_' title='Memed, My Hawk (İnce Memed, #1) by Yaşar Kemal' rel='nofollow noopener'>Memed, My Hawk</a> is a folk tale of injustice by a cruel landlord turning a young farmer's son to brigandage. At the same time he is a brigand, he is scrupulously justice, especially when dealing with the poor and the innocent. <br/><br/>"Slim Memed," as he is called, is a hero created by an author who doesn't believe in heroes. In his introduction to the New York Review Books edition, Kemal writ</span>
  <span id="freeText12387656727720561219" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14556509.Yashar_Kemal" title="Yashar Kemal" rel="nofollow noopener">Yashar Kemal</a> is probably the best known author from that most admirable of Middle-Eastern peoples: The Kurds. His <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/175545.Memed__My_Hawk___nce_Memed___1_" title="Memed, My Hawk (İnce Memed, #1) by Yaşar Kemal" rel="nofollow noopener">Memed, My Hawk</a> is a folk tale of injustice by a cruel landlord turning a young farmer's son to brigandage. At the same time he is a brigand, he is scrupulously justice, especially when dealing with the poor and the innocent. <br /><br />"Slim Memed," as he is called, is a hero created by an author who doesn't believe in heroes. In his introduction to the New York Review Books edition, Kemal writes:<blockquote>I have never believed in heroes. Even in those novels in which I focus on revolt I have tried to highlight the fact that those we call heroes are in effect instruments wielded by the people. The peoplecreate and protect these instruments and stand or fall together vwith them.</blockquote>Still and all, Kemal was to write three more books featuring Slim Memed. For the first one, he was shortlisted for the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1973. That award was won by the Australian Patrick White. I think it should have gone to Kemal.<br /><br />Kemal's villain is the landlord Abdi Agha, one of the most craven and beastly characters in all of literature. It is not until the end that Memed shoots three bullets into his chest, killing him; but he had been spiritually dead for years after Memed killed his nephew and wounded him.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12387656727720561219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1999678022" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1999678022" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1999678022?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inderjit Sanghera" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera">Inderjit Sanghera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590171394" href="/book/show/175545.Memed_My_Hawk">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1999678022" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13979997211400705139">Yashar Kemal is able to evoke the arid, yet effervescent, land of Taurus; from the ebullient sunsets to the incandescent moon-light, to the pellucid mountain slopes and parched plains or the baleful lives of the peasantry who struggle to survive beneath the oppression of feudalism, all of this is conjured up within the poetry of Kemal's prose.<br/><br/>The central character-aside from the protagonist Memed, is the country in which the story is set. Kemal. Desolate, yet beautiful, Kemal captures the decidu</span>
  <span id="freeText13979997211400705139" style="display:none">Yashar Kemal is able to evoke the arid, yet effervescent, land of Taurus; from the ebullient sunsets to the incandescent moon-light, to the pellucid mountain slopes and parched plains or the baleful lives of the peasantry who struggle to survive beneath the oppression of feudalism, all of this is conjured up within the poetry of Kemal's prose.<br /><br />The central character-aside from the protagonist Memed, is the country in which the story is set. Kemal. Desolate, yet beautiful, Kemal captures the deciduous and ethereal sunsets which illuminate the country, transmogrifying everything into a kaleidoscope of different colours, textures and tones, shedding some light on the bleakness of the peasants lives-there is something almost religious about Kemal's evocations of nature, something sacrosanct about the land which the workers toil under the turgid oppression of the landowners-even the prickly thistles are transformed in the sun-light;<br /><br />"In spring the thistles are an anaemic, pale green. A light breeze can bend them to the earth. By midsummer the first blue veins appear on the stems. Then the branches and the whole stem turn a pale blue. Later this blue grows steadily deeper, till a field, the whole boundless plain, becomes a sea of the finest blue. If a wind blows, towards sunset, the blue thistles ripple like the sea and rustle; just as the sea turns road at sunset, so do the thistles."<br /><br />At times the novel seems to be bathed in sun-light, as the never-ending brightness of the sun shines upon the land, upon the mountains, the swamps and the brooks, as colours coalesce from purple, to blue, to green and a glaring yellow. In contrast to this is the pale luminescence of the moon-light, as the world becomes a colder and darker place, but with a beauty which is more delicate and ephemeral;if sunlight transforms, then moonlight enhances and draws out the beauty of nature, as the whole world seems to be drowning  beneath a sea of melancholy as its torrents are washing over the characters.<br /><br />The lead character, Memed, acts as a kind of Robin Hood for the local peasants who are struggling under the oppression of the landlords. The primary antagonist in the novel is the cruel land-owner Abdi Agha; whilst the characters are drawn out relatively well, what is more important is what they represent; the powerless peasants, whose fickle cowardice allows Abdi Agha to dominate them, Abdi Agha and other landowners such as Ali Safa Bey, whose greed and avarice are responsible for the poverty of the peasantry and of heroic figures such as Memed, who represents the key with which to unlock the vice of oppression and to free the workers from the shackles of their masters, who constantly seek to coerce and dehumanise them.  Although Kemal is clearly sympathetic to the plight of the peasantry he is also critical of their inability to fight back and rely on others, such as Memed, to win back their lands and rights-without the active involvement of the workers. The workers will never free themselves from the tyranny of the land-owners until they themselves also begin to confront the injustice of the system which envelops them and although Kemal feels they were initially over-reliant on others to take back what is theirs, Memed's actions in some ways act as the spark which will eventually set their revolution ablaze, just as the sun is able to transform the thickles into something radiant. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13979997211400705139" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_322058264" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/322058264" />
    <a title="Val" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5271574-val"><img alt="Val" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529497498p2/5271574.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/322058264?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Val" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Val" href="/user/show/5271574-val">Val</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781860463914" href="/book/show/1448184.Memed_My_Hawk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5271574-val?shelf=world-tour">world-tour</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer322058264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12267343472222307758">This book is written in the style of a folk tale: as a series of events and adventures. Within those parameters it works very well, but it is very much a 'show not tell' style and lacking in emotional depth. (You know what people do, but very little about what they feel.)<br/>The descriptions of lives and places are detailed and easy to imagine, even if you have never seen anything of rural Turkey.<br/>The book is worth reading for the way of life it depicts and the story, just don't expect introspection.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_687996941" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/687996941" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/687996941?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590171394" href="/book/show/175545.Memed_My_Hawk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2013">read2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=around-the-world">around-the-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=location-turkey">location-turkey</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=nyrb">nyrb</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer687996941" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14464434799575770538">I already had this checked out from the library for my ongoing reading of Turkish literature when I came across a mention of the author in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/63689.The_Great_Railway_Bazaar' title='The Great Railway Bazaar by Paul Theroux' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Great Railway Bazaar</a> by Paul Theroux. I've had a great many reading coincidences lately!<br/><br/>It isn't surprising that this book was originally published in serial form. It has the distinct feel of sections, with repetition that would have been unnecessary if it had originally been one volume. Those repetitions were tiring to this reader, and I have the same be</span>
  <span id="freeText14464434799575770538" style="display:none">I already had this checked out from the library for my ongoing reading of Turkish literature when I came across a mention of the author in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/63689.The_Great_Railway_Bazaar" title="The Great Railway Bazaar by Paul Theroux" rel="nofollow noopener">The Great Railway Bazaar</a> by Paul Theroux. I've had a great many reading coincidences lately!<br /><br />It isn't surprising that this book was originally published in serial form. It has the distinct feel of sections, with repetition that would have been unnecessary if it had originally been one volume. Those repetitions were tiring to this reader, and I have the same beef with this novel as I do with many epic fantasies - too much time spent following the hero as he tromps around the hills, chasing down his foes or running from his enemies. And then when you consider that this is merely the first book of the Ince Memed series, this could spiral into the repetition of quests and heros that I'm just not personally much of a fan of. It will be perfect for some readers.<br /><br />Still, I'm glad I read it. I felt like the author gives vivid descriptions of the landscape of the eastern part of the country, and gives insight into the period of transition before World War I in how villages and farms were run. This isn't the first novel I've read from or about Turkey with large groups of people living on the run!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14464434799575770538" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1313275151" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer11788535658395871036">This book merits all the praise that it has received. It provides an outstanding view of life in the Anatolian highlands in the 1920s. The fall of the Ottman empire at the end of World War I has profoundly affected the social order in Turkey. Many of the old feudal families have lost their holdings. At the same time a class of nouveau richer has emerged and is making every effort to create a new feudalism by wresting the land holdings away from the peasants.<br/><br/>Some of the dispossessed have formed b</span>
  <span id="freeText11788535658395871036" style="display:none">This book merits all the praise that it has received. It provides an outstanding view of life in the Anatolian highlands in the 1920s. The fall of the Ottman empire at the end of World War I has profoundly affected the social order in Turkey. Many of the old feudal families have lost their holdings. At the same time a class of nouveau richer has emerged and is making every effort to create a new feudalism by wresting the land holdings away from the peasants.<br /><br />Some of the dispossessed have formed bands of brigands. These brigands quickly become clients of the large landholders and assist them to increase their holdings by intimidating those peasants who are resisting. <br /><br />Memed our hero however is a good brigand who sides with the small landowners. For many readers, having a Robin Hood type to cheer for adds considerably to the charm of the book. I find it a somewhat contrived element in what is nonetheless a masterful portrait of a forgotten but important component of Twentieth Century history.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11788535658395871036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2280831613" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2280831613" />
    <a title="Bob Newman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72341080-bob-newman"><img alt="Bob Newman" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2280831613?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Newman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Newman" href="/user/show/72341080-bob-newman">Bob Newman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590171394" href="/book/show/175545.Memed_My_Hawk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72341080-bob-newman?shelf=turkey-and-lit">turkey-and-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2280831613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10261796411074411168">Once Upon a Time in Turkey<br/><br/>Down in that fertile part of southern Anatolia called the Chukurova, where crops yielded forty-fold and deer, birds, and beetles throve, the feudal landlords, who owned entire villages, oppressed the peasants mightily. They took whatever share of the crops they desired and could beat the villagers on any whim, or even drive them from their homes. Justice was an undreamt-of luxury. For rebels, or for those who had incurred the landlords' wrath, the only alternative-besid</span>
  <span id="freeText10261796411074411168" style="display:none">Once Upon a Time in Turkey<br /><br />Down in that fertile part of southern Anatolia called the Chukurova, where crops yielded forty-fold and deer, birds, and beetles throve, the feudal landlords, who owned entire villages, oppressed the peasants mightily. They took whatever share of the crops they desired and could beat the villagers on any whim, or even drive them from their homes. Justice was an undreamt-of luxury. For rebels, or for those who had incurred the landlords' wrath, the only alternative-besides joining the Army---was to become a bandit in the mountains. The life of a bandit, though, however free, was usually short. Yashar Kemal, who grew up in this area, wrote this novel back in the 1950s; his first major work, which by now has been translated into nearly every major language and has become a modern classic.<br />Kemal introduces the life and traditions of the inhabitants of the Chukurova, a region unknown in most parts of the world. At least, he gives us a picture of the life they had in the 1920s or `30s. The novel describes the social conditions then existing there, introduces dozens of interesting, colorful characters, and also focusses on the natural environment, which by our times, has mostly disappeared. All this is done through the medium of a fast-moving, action-packed story which could be the script of a film (and may well have been, though I never saw it anywhere). Memed, a slim young man, wishes to marry Hatche, a beautiful village girl. The nasty landlord has other ideas---he wants her to marry his ugly nephew. The young lovers elope into the forest, but are surrounded by the landlord's minions. Memed draws his pistol and shoots the nephew dead, wounding the landlord. Memed winds up as a bandit, Hatche winds up in jail, and the rotten landlord has Memed's mother beaten to death. Her son swears revenge. Nomads, trackers, crazy bandit chiefs, tough peasant women, village farmers, policemen---the number of lifelike characters is endless. Memed not only turns bandit, but he becomes a Robin Hood character, a legend in his own time, who defies the prevailing feudal order and even re-distributes the landlord's fields to the tillers at one point. No wonder they loved him ! Perhaps some of Kemal's later work is deeper psychologically, perhaps his palette of colors got wider, but MEMED MY HAWK stands out as a great story written in masterful style. It is a novel about justice, a novel that treats basic human emotions in any time or place. It heralded the arrival of a major author on the world scene. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10261796411074411168" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_703804319" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/703804319" />
    <a title="Lilisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7209729-lilisa"><img alt="Lilisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1449806181p2/7209729.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/703804319?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lilisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lilisa" href="/user/show/7209729-lilisa">Lilisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590171394" href="/book/show/175545.Memed_My_Hawk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7209729-lilisa?shelf=around-the-world-2014">around-the-world-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7209729-lilisa?shelf=turkey">turkey</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7209729-lilisa?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7209729-lilisa?shelf=you-ll-love-this-one">you-ll-love-this-one</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7209729-lilisa?shelf=translated">translated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer703804319" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3768436190275213340">There's something about Turkish literature that speaks to me, and this book is no exception. Written in Turkish by Yasar Kemal, the book was originally published in 1955 and translated into English and several other languages since then. The novel follows the life of Memed, from his harsh young life in a poor village, losing his father, toiling day and night for the cruel local agha, subsequent attempts at escapes and then transition into a roving brigand, the story touches the heart and and spe</span>
  <span id="freeText3768436190275213340" style="display:none">There's something about Turkish literature that speaks to me, and this book is no exception. Written in Turkish by Yasar Kemal, the book was originally published in 1955 and translated into English and several other languages since then. The novel follows the life of Memed, from his harsh young life in a poor village, losing his father, toiling day and night for the cruel local agha, subsequent attempts at escapes and then transition into a roving brigand, the story touches the heart and and speaks to the mind. Yaser Kemal's poetic language and lyrical style create vivid splashes of color as we visualize the countryside, the stretches of thistle, the aroma of marjoram, thyme and oregano and the stench of cows and cow dung. We experience life as it was in the days before World War I, the plundering of villages by brigands and the utter control of villages by aghas who essentially stripped people of practically everything they owned. At the same time we can marvel at the beauty of the land, the traditions of the Turks and the close relationships between comrades. A well-written novel, Memed, My Hawk harkens back to days long gone and conjures up the image of the traditional hero, a hero like days long gone, is no more. A highly recommended read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3768436190275213340" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<span id="freeTextContainer13515602476335427326">J'ai lu ce livre, I have read this wonderful novel or only its beginning and to tell my enthuasiasm with it seems so difificult I heard about the writer long time ago, ma connaissance de cet auteur est de longue date, but somehow I only wished to read it 2 weeks ago when I stumbled on a French translation in the French cultural centre , the novel being Tourterelle, ma tourterelle which is a French translation of Turkish Yousufcuk Yousuf, with cedille in c in Yousoufcuk. The novel is astounding I</span>
  <span id="freeText13515602476335427326" style="display:none">J'ai lu ce livre, I have read this wonderful novel or only its beginning and to tell my enthuasiasm with it seems so difificult I heard about the writer long time ago, ma connaissance de cet auteur est de longue date, but somehow I only wished to read it 2 weeks ago when I stumbled on a French translation in the French cultural centre , the novel being Tourterelle, ma tourterelle which is a French translation of Turkish Yousufcuk Yousuf, with cedille in c in Yousoufcuk. The novel is astounding It kept me emotionally invested in for it is a tale about one region,one interesting and old crossing of different paths,culturally important, that of Tchoukourova, the land of Kurds and Turkmen neighbouring Iran, and Old Persian the region from where conquests were launched into the East, the region of brave and epic people where hospitality and the correct word have always meant everything. I 'll be brief - it is a novel about the old and new, events taking place after the First world war and its deception to the Turks, the final fall of the Empire and the advent of the rising classes ,not all honest and straightforward. It is a painful experience of this coming to terms with 2 things, 2 trends, 2 ways of speaking, 2 paths - how to stay true and live in a modern world, how to stay conscious of history and identity without being ridiculous. It is still modern, I think and can be applied in our post-modern world of XXlst century. The novel yusufcuk JYusuf is not on goodreads, at least not in this French translation but Ince Memed , yes it is<br />Memed, my Hawk, or Slim Memed, Memed a little 11 -year -old boy at the opening of this first volume and at its close a young man of 20, rebel a d a hero for freedom and justice, social justice.<br />I am not going to reveal the plot only I want to say it is highly engaged writing where the engagement takes into the account a serious Turkish situation deeply rooted in its social and cultural history. I cannot help thinking abour Grapes of Wrath by Steinbeck or Germinal or La terre by Zola while I was reading these two novels. They have the subject the people torn between their pressing needs to survive. Because they are mythical images which go back to the Old story of Myth about the promised land, tales that are nourishment of people at times of adversity.And at such times there are those capable of assuming these tales on their own , they are heroes and martyrs, they are those like Memed the Slim or Memed the Hawk who are good and childish, strong and capable of justice, never selfish and sporadic , good at their words. They are children and heroes, little Thumbs and Giants, piqued to the heart by misfortunes of others They are what people need. Child -like heroes, too perfect to be true. They are dream heroes. Beautiful tales of human existence and their dreams. This is a novel about human heart and its determination to go on to keep struggling for better life seen through in a dream torn from the told story of the land by the sea where one is prosperous in having enough to eat and free and not beaten by so'called masters, demon like half men half goats<br />This novel is a curiously and interestingly interspersed with stories and song of old times people singing and determined to push their memory further into the precarious future for the better , here Memed the slim, ;Memed my Hawk links the past with the present with the hope that the fiture will need him to rely on goodness and justice and disinterest. It is hope against dispair and here again I draw a point in common with Zola and Steinbeck and that is the point with the world.The novel presents a roamingh hero in time and space waiting to be called to fill his duty in response to people singing from memory in aid of his kind. The novel that in a legendary way sings or tells about in an epic singer's voice about hardships putting stress never to forget about what they are and continue in the struggle with the hopeful vision of the future in spite of chaos and changing times for times are always difficult ad hard but belief exists togetrher with legends that go from mouth to ears </span>
  <a data-text-id="13515602476335427326" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_982755899" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/982755899" />
    <a title="Chana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1400289-chana"><img alt="Chana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373441206p2/1400289.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chana" href="/user/show/1400289-chana">Chana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590171394" href="/book/show/175545.Memed_My_Hawk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=literature-fiction">literature-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=world-literature">world-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=turkey">turkey</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer982755899" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6783546564433122484">Memed, known as Slim Memed, is just a boy when he runs away from his village due to the cruelty of his village's Agha (a feudal lord). He returns after a number of months as he is worried about his mother. The Agha punishes them both and they nearly starve that winter. None of the villagers in the villages that this Agha control have any liking for him but pretend to out of fear. Lots of "I kiss your hands, I kiss your feet" to his face and name calling (infidel is the bad name they all like to </span>
  <span id="freeText6783546564433122484" style="display:none">Memed, known as Slim Memed, is just a boy when he runs away from his village due to the cruelty of his village's Agha (a feudal lord). He returns after a number of months as he is worried about his mother. The Agha punishes them both and they nearly starve that winter. None of the villagers in the villages that this Agha control have any liking for him but pretend to out of fear. Lots of "I kiss your hands, I kiss your feet" to his face and name calling (infidel is the bad name they all like to use when they want to insult someone) and death wishes behind his back. This is a culture that has death everywhere; through oppression like the Agha, through honor killing and through revenge killing, such as it being understandable to kill all of your enemy's family, little kids included. It is set in Turkey and gives some interesting, if basic, history that made me want to learn more. <br />Despite the fact that I couldn't really relate to the culture, I liked the story of Memed and his love for a village girl named Hatche. What goes on here is that there is already hatred in Memed's heart for the Agha, and the Agha has a hatred for Memed as well. When the Agha's nephew decides he wants to marry Hatche the marriage is set as far as the Agha and the nephew are concerned. So Memed and Hatche make plans to run away. They do, but they are tracked and caught. In the ensuring firefight the nephew is killed and the Agha is wounded, Hatche is captured and Memed runs to the mountains to become a brigand with the intention of eventually getting his revenge on the Agha and rescuing Hatche. In the meantime the Agha has coerced all the men who were with him during the firefight to say that Hatche was the one who shot and killed his nephew. Hatche is brought to prison. And the story goes from there.<br />From what I understand, the book is meant to point out the oppressions that the peasant class in Turkey was suffering, and the way the culture and politics were working to both fight or further that oppression. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6783546564433122484" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590171394" href="/book/show/175545.Memed_My_Hawk">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer377875123" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3245638641330855291">I'm heading to Turkey soon, so bought this classic as homework. First, the author's bio is a rag-to-riches story of growing up as a peasant farmer's son in pre-Attaturk rural Turkey, who becomes a professional letter writer then the nation's most famous and beloved author. The feudal society he describes is so primitive, you are jarred when a character references an automobile. The descriptions of the natural beauty of the Turkish interior are lush.<br/>Memed is oppressed all his young life by the ev</span>
  <span id="freeText3245638641330855291" style="display:none">I'm heading to Turkey soon, so bought this classic as homework. First, the author's bio is a rag-to-riches story of growing up as a peasant farmer's son in pre-Attaturk rural Turkey, who becomes a professional letter writer then the nation's most famous and beloved author. The feudal society he describes is so primitive, you are jarred when a character references an automobile. The descriptions of the natural beauty of the Turkish interior are lush.<br />Memed is oppressed all his young life by the evil landowner. Memed becomes a Robin Hood-like brigand, constantly pursuing his goal of killing the Agha who destroyed his own life, his mother's and that of his beloved. The ending is predictably tragic, but, finally, Memed accomplishes his goal. Kemal was the champion of the poor and oppressed (he was jailed for speaking out on their behalf) and this, his most famous novel, is why he won the hearts of all Turks--and this reader.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3245638641330855291" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_358746399" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/358746399" />
    <a title="Steve Chisnell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8382450-steve-chisnell"><img alt="Steve Chisnell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1332695208p2/8382450.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/358746399?book_show_action=true">Jun 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve Chisnell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve Chisnell" href="/user/show/8382450-steve-chisnell">Steve Chisnell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590171394" href="/book/show/175545.Memed_My_Hawk">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer358746399" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3983493245431417766">A chilling and fascinating narrative of rural Turkey soon after the formation of the Republic. Memed's experiences and those of his village are offered in brutal relief; the helplessness of the peasants reveals the inevitability of human ignorance; the pettiness of the bourgeoisie is starkly honest. I found interesting how key moments of dramatic resolution are sometimes avoided in the narrative (the fate of a main character, the removal of another in a single sentence without comment). It force</span>
  <span id="freeText3983493245431417766" style="display:none">A chilling and fascinating narrative of rural Turkey soon after the formation of the Republic. Memed's experiences and those of his village are offered in brutal relief; the helplessness of the peasants reveals the inevitability of human ignorance; the pettiness of the bourgeoisie is starkly honest. I found interesting how key moments of dramatic resolution are sometimes avoided in the narrative (the fate of a main character, the removal of another in a single sentence without comment). It forced me to re-examine Kemal's narrative focus and purpose; he is myth-making, perhaps, over story-telling, and for that the novel is all the more rich.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3983493245431417766" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_240297860" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/240297860" />
    <a title="Ajk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7057925-ajk"><img alt="Ajk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1322424480p2/7057925.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/240297860?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ajk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ajk" href="/user/show/7057925-ajk">Ajk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590171394" href="/book/show/175545.Memed_My_Hawk">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Turkophiles</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7057925-ajk?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7057925-ajk?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer240297860" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6041376025979522652">There's a fantastic review of Absurdistan I once read that said:<br/>Just unbutton its shirt and let it bare its chest. Like a victorious wrestler, this novel is so immodestly vigorous, so burstingly sure of its barbaric excellence, that simply by breathing, sweating and standing upright it exalts itself.<br/>And yeah, I think that applies to Memed, My Hawk a whole lot more than it applies to Shteyngart's work. It's rare when you can write a classical legend in 1955. It's a John Ford Western except that i</span>
  <span id="freeText6041376025979522652" style="display:none">There's a fantastic review of Absurdistan I once read that said:<br />Just unbutton its shirt and let it bare its chest. Like a victorious wrestler, this novel is so immodestly vigorous, so burstingly sure of its barbaric excellence, that simply by breathing, sweating and standing upright it exalts itself.<br />And yeah, I think that applies to Memed, My Hawk a whole lot more than it applies to Shteyngart's work. It's rare when you can write a classical legend in 1955. It's a John Ford Western except that it is just as relevant to today as it was in the '50s and unlike the John Fords, it took place in contemporary times. Yashar Kemal owns. He made me forget my Turkey and start living in his for a few hundred pages. I'm not sure what else I could say but that.<br /><br />The book is about the titular character, believe it or not. As a boy, Memed runs from home, finds the world outside is pretty interesting, but ultimately is drawn back home. His family, seemingly longstanding enemies of Abdi Agha, the ruler of Degirmenoluk and four other villages, is punished for his transgressions. Years after this continual punishment he runs away with his beloved. That works out poorly. He then takes to the mountains and becomes the bravest brigand the Cukurova has seen.<br /><br />Although it itself is a legend, it also discusses how legends are made. The tiny, boyish, Memed becomes a monster to his enemies and a haughty animal to his companions. The book is about how expansive Turkey is, it makes the land east of Ankara seem as expansive as the Midwest plains that hold the stars I still recognize as home. I'm not nearly as clever a book reviewer to discuss how Kemal writes how he does. So just read it and enjoy the ride.<br /><br />Kemal was a complicated figure. And while you hear some biographers say that with concern, for me, I think it just means that he was interesting. Oh, gee, wow, he didn't have a clear-cut position on socialism. That's not being difficult, its avoiding the drab talking points of his era. The man's story is fantastic. He loved telling stories as a kid growing up in rural (RURAL!) Turkey, but he was distraught when he woke up one day and couldn't remember the words to his favorite story. He decided he would have to learn to write in order to be a better story teller. There are then stories of him getting a job in the library so he could just read all day and other such fantastic things. They matter less as factual vignettes than as a mythical image of Yashar the author from the hills. The Nation's article on him is fantastic and is a must-read. I went from "OK, I ought to read Kemal" to "I must read him" solely because of that article.<br /><br />I love how far removed the book is from Istanbul. So many foreigners coming to Turkey focus on Istanbul. And I admit to yes, living here and yes, finding it quite nice. All the same, when I read about how "Istanbul thrives as the new party capital of Europe" all I can think of is "Istanbul is pretty much the most boring city in Europe." Oooh, more couchsurfers looking for love (and/or friction)? Sounds great! Bridge East-West! Live your exotic fantasy without having to learn a word! The fact is Turkey is so much more than Istanbul. And now that Istanbul has welcomed a generation of internal immigrants, the rest of Turkey is coming to Istanbul. This has caused far more panic and fear than a welcoming of a whole new side of cool.<br /><br />In this book, near none of the characters have names that smack of Turkey. Ok, sure, there's a Memet, an Ibrahim. But even "Ali" seems rare these days. And I have yet to come across an Iraz, a Hatche. Let alone an Abdi or Jaffar. Memed comes to "the big city" that is likely smaller than Adana. Maras is the big town. Nomads make up a significant plot point. We're a long ways from Reina, and I love it. This is as much Turkey as anything else.<br /><br />While Turkey wrestles or whatever with its identity, it gets turned into an Evren v. Erdogan affair. The rise of Turkey's east, it's businessmen and its politicians is just as important. And it goes far beyond the Kurdish and Armenian plot points. Kemal gives life to the humans on the fringe that have made Turkey grow in its Republican days. He has created the image of the Turkish lower class that Fatih Akin and Nuri Bilge Ceylan have latched on to.<br /><br />More to the point, Memed, My Hawk reads like a campfire tale told by Cool Grandpa Yashar. There are good guys and bad guys. Beautiful women and wise old crones. For being entirely too realistic, it reads like magical fantasy. Taunts at gunfights, single-minded men, families pushed to their limits...these are not the things of history, these are the tales of now.<br /><br />Memed, My Hawk is about Turkey and, to paraphrase a friend, says "Bastards!" to Istanbul, says "Bastards!" to Ankara. It thumbs its nose at the foreign correspondent, it would do worse to the modern-day blogger if one could imagine Memed understanding computers. Kemal writes about Turkey and is wrapped up in its legends, its myths, its identity. The American coming in to Turkey can never understand it, especially not from a stone's throw from the Bosphorus. It doesn't matter how strongly they arch their eyebrows, how perfectly they tent their fingers. Kemal, one can say, claims Turkey for the Turks through literature (though he may hate to hear that expression used for him).<br /><br />All we do is try to explain our impressions to other foreigners. The narratives that we all try to force Turkey into, the way we cram-pack Menderes and Davutoglu into one timeline, are all ways of reflecting what we want Turkey to be. Meanwhile, here's Yashar Bey, flaunting the Turkey we all wish we could capture.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6041376025979522652" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2785886630" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12283134254319092379">A simple but yet intricately detailed story which was quite long but over all a pleasant and rewarding read. I was thankful for the satisfying ending. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1551512392" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1551512392" />
    <a title="Soren" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34121152-soren"><img alt="Soren" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525869495p2/34121152.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1551512392?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Soren" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Soren" href="/user/show/34121152-soren">Soren</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/4254323-memed-my-hawk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34121152-soren?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1551512392" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1431504975751450951">This book is awesome. Yes, the plot follows the basic structure of a revenge tale, but there's a lot more going on. First of all, the descriptions of natural beauty are really wonderful, especially as they are incorporated to the zoom-in style that Kemal uses at the start of many of the chapters where he gives us a beautiful nature scene, zooms in to a character whose importance is unknown to us, then zooms in again to show us how this all connects back to the main character and the central plot</span>
  <span id="freeText1431504975751450951" style="display:none">This book is awesome. Yes, the plot follows the basic structure of a revenge tale, but there's a lot more going on. First of all, the descriptions of natural beauty are really wonderful, especially as they are incorporated to the zoom-in style that Kemal uses at the start of many of the chapters where he gives us a beautiful nature scene, zooms in to a character whose importance is unknown to us, then zooms in again to show us how this all connects back to the main character and the central plot. <br /><br />I love any book that examines the question of what it means to be a good person, and especially what it means to be a good leader, without arriving at definite answers or being preachy. This book does that wonderfully and we're left in awe of Memed, even though he does a lot of bad things. I also really enjoyed how we get to see the conflicting ethos of different characters depending on where they're from - Big Osman with his oldschool values, Abdi's (and Ali Safa's) selfishness, Durdu's nihilism, the villagers' mob mentality, Lame Ali's need to follow a trail once he's started, Sergeant Rejep's indiscernible code, Sergeant Asim's pivotal choice at the end of the book, and, of course, Memed's sense of justice. <br /><br />Two other random details: I love how much description Kemal provides of the smells of what's going on, and also how short the descriptions of the battles are. If you read more contemporary stuff, you expect action scenes to be longer and flashier. But, really, the action here is just a plot device and it doesn't enhance our understanding of the characters, so Kemal give us only what we need, and then continues with his wonderful story.<br /><br />This book is really highly regarded and one of the best-known Turkish novels, so I was supremely please that it lived up to its reputation. I definitely recommend this to anybody who wants to read a fun, gratifying adventure soaked with complexity and nuance.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1431504975751450951" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Rizvee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76098884-rizvee"><img alt="Rizvee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591476129p2/76098884.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2784826282?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rizvee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rizvee" href="/user/show/76098884-rizvee">Rizvee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
